<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer15890235132262361455">Western Pennsylvania, 1974. On a snowy winter afternoon, the winter of his parents' breakup, Arthur Parkinson's high school band practice is interrupted by the sound of gunshots. Too close for deer hunting, it is the sound of the murder of Annie Marchand. Once Arthur's babysitter, and the object of his childhood admiration, Annie is a young woman for whom life didn't turn</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15890235132262361455">Western Pennsylvania, 1974. On a snowy winter afternoon, the winter of his parents' breakup, Arthur Parkinson's high school band practice is interrupted by the sound of gunshots. Too close for deer hunting, it is the sound of the murder of Annie Marchand. Once Arthur's babysitter, and the object of his childhood admiration, Annie is a young woman for whom life didn't turn out quite right, who could find no one to blame, and who could not keep herself, or her loved ones, from harm. With exquisite feeling and perfect pitch, Snow Angels weaves together two haunting stories: Arthur's account of how his family fell apart and everything went wrong the year he turned fifteen, and the shifting-focus story of Annie Marchand and the broken life she cannot seem to reassemble - a story that will draw Arthur into its deepening eddy as it nears an inevitable conclusion.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15890235132262361455">...more</a>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_30893116">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/30893116" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1232712-matt" class="left imgcol" title="Matt"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1404077158p2/1232712.jpg" alt="Matt" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/30893116?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 17, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1232712-matt" class="user" title="Matt">Matt</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/376630.Snow_Angels" title="Paperback 9780312422769" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer30893116">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13728005230762301340">This is the second novel I've read by Stewart O'Nan, after <em>Last Night at the Lobster</em>. While I enjoyed <em>Lobster</em>," it really is a trifle compared to <em>Snow Angels</em>, which is an immensely quiet, powerful book. Essentially, it tells the story of two relationships, both disintegrating, set against the backdrop of rural Pennsylvania in the 70's (my onetime hometown of Bloomington, MN makes an appearance, as does that pansy Fran Tarkenton, getting chased down by the Steelers D). <br /><br />In it's way, <em>Snow Angels</em> re</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13728005230762301340">This is the second novel I've read by Stewart O'Nan, after <em>Last Night at the Lobster</em>. While I enjoyed <em>Lobster</em>," it really is a trifle compared to <em>Snow Angels</em>, which is an immensely quiet, powerful book. Essentially, it tells the story of two relationships, both disintegrating, set against the backdrop of rural Pennsylvania in the 70's (my onetime hometown of Bloomington, MN makes an appearance, as does that pansy Fran Tarkenton, getting chased down by the Steelers D). <br /><br />In it's way, <em>Snow Angels</em> reminded me of Edith Wharton's <em>Ethan Frome</em>, with the small town and big secrets; the insular lives; the omnipresent snow and cold; the lonely single road that leads to Tragedy. The story is told, in part, by Arthur Parkinson, who relates the story in flashback. He tells his own story - of his parent's divorce, of his first love, Lila, and his memories of Annie, his one-time babysitter who is murdered (you know this on the first page, so it's not a spoiler)- in the first person. He narrates the second strand of the story, the relationship of Annie and her estranged husband Glenn, in the third-person (you know he is the narrator because, at one point, he interjects himself, to powerful effect, into the third-person strand). <br /><br />O'Nan's writing is simple and direct, yet he manages, with great efficiency, to describe indelible characters. His description of the breakdown of Arthur's parent's marriage is spot on. I say this from experience, having seen my own parents get divorced. He eerily captures the feeling of a child torn between two warring factions in the most bitter of all conflicts: the conflict that occurs when love turns to hate (see, e.g., <em>Star Wars</em>). The scenes with Arthur warily dealing with his mother and father are among my favorites. The first-person narration adds to the strength of these sections. <br /><br />The story line with Annie and Glenn is a bit more prosaic. On the first page we learn that Annie is murdered. The rest of the novel tells you how we got to that point. Needless to say, it has to do with some of the choices she makes, choices relating to the fickle emotions emanating from our chest cavities. While believably told, it is a bit obvious. We've all read plenty of murder stories and know how we're supposed to feel; it's more interesting to read a detailed story about a divorce, with its emotional complexity, because even though it is far more familiar to our experience, it doesn't often get explored. <br /><br />This is not a book with happy endings. The theme, reiterated over and over, is how we come to part from the people we love. It's a subject I've spent the last year mulling. Once upon a time I was engaged to a girl I thought I'd see every day for the rest of my life. Now, there are times I can't remember her face, or the sound of her voice, or the way she smelled (and they say smell is one of those memories that last the longest). I always wonder at that: how you go to a wedding, everyone is happy, smiling groom, blushing bride, weeping parents. Oaths before God and all that. And somehow, in some way, so many of those perfect unions are rent asunder. When it is all over, and there is nothing but ashes, you start to wonder if love is a cosmic joke that lures us with delusions of infinity, then smacks us with the reality that it's as fleeting as an evening shadow. <br /><br />The theme is stated succinctly near the end, after Arthur's father tries to reconcile with his mother, and his mother rebuffs him. She tries to explain to Arthur why she did what she did: <br /><br /><blockquote>'I hope you understand what just happened,' she said, 'and why it has to be this way...' <br />'I guess,' I said, and it was not an evasion. Because though it was already happening to me, I could not see how I would ever come to hate the people I loved. Yet at the same time I could do nothing to stop it, and that would not change for a very long time.</blockquote><br /><br />When you are done reading it, you will probably want a hug, so it is best to read this in a room full of people who will be willing to hug you, or perhaps with a dog, though the dog will not hug you back, unless you've trained it to do so. You should also hug Stewart O'Nan if you see him, because I think he needs one. <br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13728005230762301340">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_263557166">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/263557166" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3025547-mike" class="left imgcol" title="Mike"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1446412780p2/3025547.jpg" alt="Mike" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/263557166?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 16, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3025547-mike" class="user" title="Mike">Mike</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/528466.Snow_Angels" title="Hardcover 9780385475747" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Mike by: </span>
      Jeffrey Keeten, Goodreads friend and pal

              <a href="/review/list/3025547-mike?shelf=children" class="actionLinkLite">children</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3025547-mike?shelf=20th-century" class="actionLinkLite">20th-century</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3025547-mike?shelf=adolesence" class="actionLinkLite">adolesence</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3025547-mike?shelf=family" class="actionLinkLite">family</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3025547-mike?shelf=parents-and-children" class="actionLinkLite">parents-and-children</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3025547-mike?shelf=2012" class="actionLinkLite">2012</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer263557166">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12862051255771437352"><strong>Snow Angels, <em>Stewart O'Nan's novel of family life</em></strong><br /><br />“All happy families are alike; each unhappy family is unhappy in its own way.” <br />? <a title="Leo Tolstoy" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/85.Leo_Tolstoy">Leo Tolstoy</a>, <a title="Anna Karenina by Leo Tolstoy" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5685.Anna_Karenina_">Anna Karenina</a><br /><br />It is 1974 in Butler, Pennsylvania. Arthur is fourteen. His older sister, Astrid is in military service, flying high over Russia, photographing bits and pieces of a very cold country in a very cold war. But the real war is back in Butler, on the home front.<br /><br />Arthur's mother and father are splitting up. His mother explains to him that she loved</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12862051255771437352"><strong>Snow Angels, <em>Stewart O'Nan's novel of family life</em></strong><br /><br />“All happy families are alike; each unhappy family is unhappy in its own way.” <br />? <a title="Leo Tolstoy" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/85.Leo_Tolstoy">Leo Tolstoy</a>, <a title="Anna Karenina  by Leo Tolstoy" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5685.Anna_Karenina_">Anna Karenina</a><br /><br />It is 1974 in Butler, Pennsylvania. Arthur is fourteen. His older sister, Astrid is in military service, flying high over Russia, photographing bits and pieces of a very cold country in a very cold war. But the real war is back in Butler, on the home front.<br /><br />Arthur's mother and father are splitting up. His mother explains to him that she loved another man and his father could not forgive her. She adds that she was not the villainess, Arty's father had his own women on the side.<br /><br />At the same time, Annie Marchand, the perfect babysitter who had watched Arthur and Astrid while their parents lived out the motions of happiness in a marriage, has problems of her own. Life hasn't turned out well for the pretty babysitter, now grown, married, and the mother of a beautiful little girl, Tara.<br /><br />Annie's problem is her husband Glenn. He's not a bad sort. In fact, he's so damned nice. He's the excited puppy, bouncing from job to job that he can never keep. No matter how good his intentions, Glenn finds reasons to be late for work. Too much booze. Too little interest. Another job hunt. Annie soldiers on as the family breadwinner, a waitress at the club, overlooking the golf course.<br /><br />It is a cold winter afternoon. Mr. Chervinick has the band practicing out on the soccer field, preparing for the last half-time show of the football season. Arthur, in the trombone line, a highschool freshman, numbly marches through the drill routine, obliques and a whirlwind maneuver that Chervinick calls the tornado, a movement the band never can perform to their director's satisfaction.<br /><br />As Jimmy Buffet sings, "There ain't to reason to hurricane season." A tornado is something that can't be controlled, nor can a high school band replicate its movements on a practice field.<br /><br />During their attempt to swirl and twirl across the practice field, gunshots ring out. This is where our story begins.<br /><br />From that point on, O'Nan deftly weaves the stories of Arthur and Annie's families. There is a terrible beauty in the symmetry of the death of two marriages. After stormy screaming matches between his mother and father, Arthur's family dissolves in a whimper. Annie's ends in those gunshots that reverberate over the practice field.<br /><br />Moving between the voices of Arthur, Annie, her bad boy lover, Brock, and husband Glenn, slowly spiralling into madness, <a title="Snow Angels by Stewart O'Nan" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/528466.Snow_Angels">Snow Angels</a> sings with the power of a Greek chorus in building to an unavoidably tragic conclusion.<br /><br />While O'Nan weaves all the strands of a spider's intricate web of emotion, interspersed are moments of dark humor as Arthur suffers the consequences of his parents' divorce. No longer able to sustain two homes equivalent to their former household, Arthur finds himself living in the most efficient of efficiency apartments, a former retreat for ministers, now converted into family apartments. The chapel has been demolished, but its foundation stands as a reminder of its former role. It is a place Arthur and his bus mates laughed at on their way to school. It is the home of twin sisters Lily and Lila, also the butt of Arthur and his friends crude humor. Now Arthur waits for the bus with Lily and Lila, slowly finding himself attracted to Lila. He wonders what she would look like without her glasses and what her body might be like underneath the home sewn clothes she wears.<br /><br />We follow Arthur through obtaining his learner's permit, his father's sporadic and awkward weekend visits. Arthur's father teaches him to drive in a battered car belonging to his paternal aunt. His mother, also a waitress, as is Annie, has kept the family Country Squire, the quintessential automotive image of the perfect family's perfect automobile, a reminder of family vacations during happier days. These days cannot be recreated. They can only be remembered.<br /><br />Astrid appears as a distant voice on telephone calls from Germany. She is removed from the cold war at home. Although she asks Arthur if he would like her to take leave and come home as he doesn't seem to be taking care of their mother, clearly she has no intention of doing so. Arthur takes satisfaction in knowing where her secret stash of weed is, the pipes, the bongs, the papers. That will show her.<br /><br />We watch as Arthur sets out on his trek to adulthood, desperately trying to arrive there in the arms of Lila. She's very nice without her glasses on. And, oh God, the bra is off. Oh, God, what is beneath that awful binding garment is beyond Arthur's wildest dreams. And the wheels on the bus go round and round. What a reminiscence--the opening of the old television show, "Ben Casey." Perhaps you remember it. "Man. Woman. Birth. Death. Infinity." <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?feature=endscreen&amp;NR=1&amp;v=10Jklu-kd8k" target="_blank">http://www.youtube.com/watch?feature=...</a> That's O'Nan's marvelous novel in a nutshell. <br /><br />Snow angels. I've made them as a child. I've watched my children make them. Giddy moments of the exhilaration of being a child and alive. In this novel, snow angels are not symbols of happiness but the bleak harbingers of unhappiness and a tornado of death and violence that will forever haunt the reader.<br /><br />Yes, each unhappy family is unhappy in its own way. This novel almost makes you wonder if there is such a thing as reincarnation.<br /><br />Born in 1961, Stewart O'Nan took his MFA from Cornell in 1992. His short story collection, <a title="In the Walled City by Stewart O'Nan" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1083292.In_the_Walled_City">In the Walled City</a>, was awarded the Druse Heinz in 1993. His manuscript of <a title="Snow Angels by Stewart O'Nan" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/528466.Snow_Angels">Snow Angels</a> captured the first Pirate's Alley Faulkner Prize for the Novel, awarded by the Pirate's Alley Faulkner Society of New Orleans in 1993. The novel was published in January, 1994. <br /><br />O'Nan's latest novel is <a title="The Odds A Love Story by Stewart O'Nan" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11797352.The_Odds_A_Love_Story">The Odds</a>, out January, 2012. I'll have Mr. O'Nan sign that at the Alabama Booksmith in Homewood, Alabama on January 23, this week, when I'll also respectfully ask him to sign my copy of this novel and his award winning collection of short fiction, <em>In the Walled City</em>.<br /><br /><strong>Rating: A solid 4.5 out of 5</strong></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12862051255771437352">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_75610960">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/75610960" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1943394-kelly" class="left imgcol" title="Kelly "><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1369524509p2/1943394.jpg" alt="Kelly " /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/75610960?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 22, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1943394-kelly" class="user" title="Kelly ">Kelly </a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/528466.Snow_Angels" title="Hardcover 9780385475747" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1943394-kelly?shelf=2010" class="actionLinkLite">2010</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1943394-kelly?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer75610960">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15584294434493900055">After reading <a title="Last Night at the Lobster by Stewart O'Nan" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/673915.Last_Night_at_the_Lobster">Last Night at the Lobster</a>, I became a fan of Stewart O'Nan and knew that I would be reading more from him. <em>Snow Angels</em> is the first novel O'Nan wrote, and my second read from this author. The story is narrated by Arthur Parkinson, who was 14 years old during the winter of 1974 and living in the small town of Butler, Pennsylvania. This is the winter that his parents separate and his family falls apart. He and his mom move into a run-down little apartment, his dad is dating someone n</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15584294434493900055">After reading <a title="Last Night at the Lobster by Stewart O'Nan" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/673915.Last_Night_at_the_Lobster">Last Night at the Lobster</a>, I became a fan of Stewart O'Nan and knew that I would be reading more from him. <em>Snow Angels</em> is the first novel O'Nan wrote, and my second read from this author. The story is narrated by Arthur Parkinson, who was 14 years old during the winter of 1974 and living in the small town of Butler, Pennsylvania. This is the winter that his parents separate and his family falls apart. He and his mom move into a run-down little apartment, his dad is dating someone new, his mom is angry and depressed and coming undone, and Arthur's sister Astrid is calling from her station in Germany every so often to blame Arthur for not doing more to "fix" the situation. Simultaneously, we learn the story of Annie Marchand, Arthur's childhood babysitter, who is down on her luck with things continuously getting worse. The parallel storylines lead up to the event of her murder one cold snowy evening while Arthur is at band practice and everyone hears the gunshot.<br /><br />The only reason I'm giving this one 4 stars instead of 5 is because I got confused with the timeline early on. I eventually figured it out, but it made everything feel a bit disjointed in the beginning. Basically, Arthur is now an adult and is looking back on what happened during the winter of 74, as he and Astrid are visiting their old hometown for Christmas. This is stated in Ch.1, and then Ch.2 begins in October of 74 and switches perspectives between Arthur, Annie, and a few people connected to Annie as things lead up to her murder. I didn't really feel this was quite clear enough in the beginning; but then again, I may have been half-asleep when I began the book.<br /><br /><br />ETA: I'm changing my rating to 5 stars, because the story has remained with me all day after finishing the book. The story really sucks you in and gives you a great understanding of why the characters are the way that they are. The setting is fantastically described, and the snowy weather is as gray as the characters' circumstances. It's not a feel-good book by any means, but it is complex and meaningful and realistic. <br /><br />**I just realized this was made into a movie in 2007, starring Kate Beckinsale. Can't wait to watch it!**</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15584294434493900055">...more</a>

          </span>

      <a href="/user/show/1883816-barbara" class="user" title="Barbara">Barbara</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer127736384">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17832683847995255285">Arthur Parkinson was 14 when his former, beloved babysitter, Annie, was murdered. This we discover at the outset of this book. The story is narrated throughout by the adult Arthur and some of the other prime characters. It focuses on the period surrounding this slaying and also the traumatic divorce of the young Artie. <br /><br />O'Nan proficiently conveyed the emotional impact that each person was experiencing. His handling of the actions and behavior of this adolescent boy was particularly vivid and astu</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17832683847995255285">Arthur Parkinson was 14 when his former, beloved babysitter, Annie, was murdered. This we discover at the outset of this book. The story is narrated throughout by the adult Arthur and some of the other prime characters. It focuses on the period surrounding this slaying and also the traumatic divorce of the young Artie. <br /><br />O'Nan proficiently conveyed the emotional impact that each person was experiencing. His handling of the actions and behavior of this adolescent boy was particularly vivid and astute. For the most part, the characters in this novel, residents of a small Western Pennsyvania town, lived grueling, hand-to-mouth existences. Each seemed to lack the inner resources to deal with problematic interpersonal relationships,problem solving, or worse, with profound tragedy. The latter seemed to prevail in this township.<br /><br />Snow Angels is skillfully conceived and, for the most part, interest is well fueled by O'Nan's writing, but I could not gain enough enthusiasm to rate it more than 3 stars. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17832683847995255285">...more</a>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer72240906">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5198037580795747013">My first impression of this book was that it was meant for 6th graders. The font size used was huge and made it appear as if it had 10 words per page.<br /><br />Once I started reading it, I realized I wasn't too far off. While the subject matter is too much of a downer for a nine year old, the way the story was written would have made them feel right at home. The sentences were choppy and had no weight to them.<br /><br />There were so many missed opportunities that could have made this book a knockout, but were simpl</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5198037580795747013">My first impression of this book was that it was meant for 6th graders. The font size used was huge and made it appear as if it had 10 words per page.<br /><br />Once I started reading it, I realized I wasn't too far off. While the subject matter is too much of a downer for a nine year old, the way the story was written would have made them feel right at home. The sentences were choppy and had no weight to them.<br /><br />There were so many missed opportunities that could have made this book a knockout, but were simply washed away. The deaths of several characters were described in at most 2 simply written paragraphs. I was left with a feeling of wanting more (or really SOMETHING) and getting handed simply a statement.<br /><br />You don't get a sense of love or hatred for any of the characters. While a lot of situations were sad, they were just that. Sad. Then you moved on. You didn't relate to the characters just the situation, which when it comes to reading a 300 page book, would help.<br /><br />I was disappointed by this book as it was recommended by someone who reads a massive amount of books. This book will definitely be sold at the next yard sale. No reason to keep it.</span>

      <a href="/user/show/11869072-jeanette" class="user" title="Jeanette">Jeanette</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1186208284">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6453565863999720754">It really equates to a 4 star in the writing and the balance of the duo marriage stories. But my enjoyment of it was below 3. O'Nan writes actual common folks at a 5 star level, consistently. I love his work much more when he also meanders into whimsy and minutia of era and place, and never as much when he hits his deepest notes of tragedy. This is prime tragedy. Recognizing all of these individuals, there were few that caught my concern- and none that grabbed my affection. Flawed, flawed people</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6453565863999720754">It really equates to a 4 star in the writing and the balance of the duo marriage stories. But my enjoyment of it was below 3. O'Nan writes actual common folks at a 5 star level, consistently. I love his work much more when he also meanders into whimsy and minutia of era and place, and never as much when he hits his deepest notes of tragedy. This is prime tragedy. Recognizing all of these individuals, there were few that caught my concern- and none that grabbed my affection. Flawed, flawed people making circular decisions in ever downward spirals. Life reflected for sure. Very depressing to read for me. I wanted to grab Annie by her hair to yank her out of her mindlessness and cruelty cocoon into some measure of semi-adult self-identity. Weak and selfish people with lack of sensibility to moral values or emotionally sound focus dominate this novel. They're all predominantly reactive and most rudderless to knowledge of their own purpose. To such an extent that I could not get embedded in their eventual outcomes, so it was a 2.5 in enjoyment for the read. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6453565863999720754">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_262956908">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/262956908" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/780667-tripp" class="left imgcol" title="Tripp"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1361236016p2/780667.jpg" alt="Tripp" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/262956908?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 16, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/780667-tripp" class="user" title="Tripp">Tripp</a>

          added it


          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/376630.Snow_Angels" title="Paperback 9780312422769" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/780667-tripp?shelf=1st-person" class="actionLinkLite">1st-person</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/780667-tripp?shelf=3rd-person" class="actionLinkLite">3rd-person</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/780667-tripp?shelf=omniscient" class="actionLinkLite">omniscient</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/780667-tripp?shelf=past-tense" class="actionLinkLite">past-tense</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/780667-tripp?shelf=present-tense" class="actionLinkLite">present-tense</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/780667-tripp?shelf=multiple-pov" class="actionLinkLite">multiple-pov</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/780667-tripp?shelf=multiple-tenses" class="actionLinkLite">multiple-tenses</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer262956908">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11677737270144789303">A good book to read if you want to see how elastic point-of-view can become in the hands of a skillful writer. O'Nan easily moves from the rotating third-person--often distant to the point of omniscience--present tense viewpoint of one storyline, that of the multiple tragedies that befall Glenn and Annie Marchand, to the first-person, past tense account of 14-year-old Arthur Parkinson, narrating at a remove of some decades the more ordinary tragedy of his parents' unraveling marriage after 23 ye</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11677737270144789303">A good book to read if you want to see how elastic point-of-view can become in the hands of a skillful writer. O'Nan easily moves from the rotating third-person--often distant to the point of omniscience--present tense viewpoint of one storyline, that of the multiple tragedies that befall Glenn and Annie Marchand, to the first-person, past tense account of 14-year-old Arthur Parkinson, narrating at a remove of some decades the more ordinary tragedy of his parents' unraveling marriage after 23 years. All of this takes place in a marvelously evoked late 1970s setting of small town Pennsylvania.<br /><br />At the book's midpoint, O'Nan delivers the first of two almost unbearable scenes, proving the maxim that extreme emotion is best portrayed by paradoxically using neutral language. He also shifts briefly into the future tense, moving to the story-present of middle-aged Arthur as he looks back on this searing moment:<br /><br /><blockquote>The hunt has spread across the interstate to the middle school grounds. Trucks file by the flares, the troopers' orange-coned flashlights. The Army Reserve has promised two squads if this should go until tomorrow.</blockquote><br /><blockquote>Yet it will not be any of these searchers who finds Tara, but a fourteen-year-old from the high school marching band, small for his size, generally ignored, in fact, myself, Arthur Parkinson, who, because she is dead, will not be a hero--will not, years from now, even be remembered around town as the one who found her--but who with Annie and Glenn and Brock and May and Frank and Olive and Clare and Barb, will find Tara again and again throughout his life and never ever lose her.</blockquote><br /><br />I love this passage. It's as if O'Nan has distilled a pure essence of sadness and decanted it onto the page.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11677737270144789303">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer145860079">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16078990600763446218">Quick 10 minute review: O'Nan is always able to write with a certain endearing realistic aesthetic that allows the reader to relate to some aspect or character in every one of his novels. In his debut, Snow Angels, he basically examines the 3 different stages of a failed relationship. The story is told through the eyes of Arthur Parkinson and juxtaposes between his life: falling in love for the first time, getting high with his best friend, and dealing with his parents' recent separation, to the</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16078990600763446218">Quick 10 minute review: O'Nan is always able to write with a certain endearing realistic aesthetic that allows the reader to relate to some aspect or character in every one of his novels. In his debut, Snow Angels, he basically examines the 3 different stages of a failed relationship. The story is told through the eyes of Arthur Parkinson and juxtaposes between his life: falling in love for the first time, getting high with his best friend, and dealing with his parents' recent separation, to the characters Annie and Glenn who recently divorced. <br /><br />Each character in the novel is flawed in some way. While some are more flawed than others, you find a way to relate to them. O'Nan writes with such emotional intensity that we always find ourselves caring about the characters he creates, despite the bad things they've done. No one is perfect and O'Nan expresses that. <br /><br />Quick Summation: It is a very captivating novel that is easy to immerse yourself in the flawed yet beautiful world of.</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1176321801?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 27, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/10735195-marc-maurer" class="user" title="Marc Maurer">Marc Maurer</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/376630.Snow_Angels" title="Paperback 9780312422769" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1176321801">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1474803731744040527">This book is set in my home town. Well, not exactly since it's western Pennsylvania and I'm from central Minnesota. But the world is the same: a small town with nothing to do but keep an eye on your neighbors. Getting too involved with them is bound to bring gossip, but there isn't anybody else around.<br /><br />I'm not really sure what to make of this book. It's a little book of unhappy people doing unhappy things, and it doesn't really resolve so much as it just stops.<br /><br />The action is framed as a reminiscen</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1474803731744040527">This book is set in my home town. Well, not exactly since it's western Pennsylvania and I'm from central Minnesota. But the world is the same: a small town with nothing to do but keep an eye on your neighbors. Getting too involved with them is bound to bring gossip, but there isn't anybody else around.<br /><br />I'm not really sure what to make of this book. It's a little book of unhappy people doing unhappy things, and it doesn't really resolve so much as it just stops.<br /><br />The action is framed as a reminiscence of a young adult Arthur as he and his sister drive to his mother's house. He thinks back on two time periods, during his parents' separation, and after the murder of his childhood babysitter. Those two time periods gradually link up since they happen during the same winter. The framing story doesn't really go anywhere; the main body of the book is the winter of 1974. I don't think the book would have suffered for leaving out the frame. The book ends at Christmas 1974, and we never get any of adult Arthur's perspective on the events--just the teenage Arthur's view of things.<br /><br />The book was well-written, and I found myself interested in each of the characters. There wasn't enough depth here, though, to make me expect that I will spend time thinking about the book once I finish this review. It's a story of people trying their best, and failing. It's very human in that way--that's something you could say about a lot of people.<br /><br />The point of view is strange--it shifts between third-person omniscient when talking about Annie, and first-person when talking about Arthur's life. I think this works in the book's attempt to draw parallels between Annie and Glen's failed marriage, Arthur's parents' failing marriage, and his own blooming relationship with Lily (a not-yet-failed relationship).</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1474803731744040527">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1449978515">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13665898321200355619">This is the author's debut novel. His attention to minute, believable detail and the ability to get inside his characters' heads is already clearly evident. From page one, we know that narrator Arthur's former babysitter and first crush, Annie, is murdered. The rest of the book tells us how and why. Arthur is also navigating freshman year of high school and dealing with the breakup of his parents' marriage. These are all unremarkable people living rather bleak lives, told in an objective and non</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13665898321200355619">This is the author's debut novel. His attention to minute, believable detail and the ability to get inside his characters' heads is already clearly evident. From page one, we know that narrator Arthur's former babysitter and first crush, Annie, is murdered. The rest of the book tells us how and why. Arthur is also navigating freshman year of high school and dealing with the breakup of his parents' marriage. These are all unremarkable people living rather bleak lives, told in an objective and non-judgmental way. I love his writing style and can always find something familiar or relate-able in his characters and settings. If you're looking for a feel-good novel, though, this isn't it.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1157521662">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1157521662" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/19511519-jennyb" class="left imgcol" title="Jennyb"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1366248515p2/19511519.jpg" alt="Jennyb" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1157521662?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 05, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/19511519-jennyb" class="user" title="Jennyb">Jennyb</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/376630.Snow_Angels" title="Paperback 9780312422769" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1157521662">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5096869610234610232">I generally like Stewart O'Nan, but I found this book disappointing. Maybe it's one of his earlier efforts -- I wouldn't call it amateurish, but while he covers some of the same themes as in later novels, here they seem more vague and unfinished. It is basically two intersecting stories: one about teenager Arthur Parkinson handling his parents' divorce, and a related one about tragic events that happen to Arthur's former babysitter, Annie Marchand.<br /><br />Annie's story has more action and interest than</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5096869610234610232">I generally like Stewart O'Nan, but I found this book disappointing. Maybe it's one of his earlier efforts -- I wouldn't call it amateurish, but while he covers some of the same themes as in later novels, here they seem more vague and unfinished. It is basically two intersecting stories: one about teenager Arthur Parkinson handling his parents' divorce, and a related one about tragic events that happen to Arthur's former babysitter, Annie Marchand.<br /><br />Annie's story has more action and interest than Arthur's, in my opinion, but it's difficult to write much about it without spoilers giving everything away. So, let me stick to the margins that won't ruin the story for readers. <br /><br />Even if this is an early effort, no one does cold like O'Nan. Many of his stories are set in wintry, cold and snowy locales, and his description of them admirably permeates the atmosphere of the whole story. You feel the dim white winter sun lacking the power to warm, see the stark lines of barren trees covered in snow, and sense everywhere the death and biting cold that winter brings. Reading about these elements in O'Nan's fiction, you feel them as qualities that seep into the characters, as much as they describe the landscape. O'Nan also chooses marginal, dying, former industrial towns, and makes you know the pinch of poverty and hopelessness in those places. In this case, his location is Butler PA, a nowhere town in the shadow of Pittsburgh, known for being Ice Box of the State, since it's consistently the coldest location in Western PA. <br /><br />In the beginning of Arthur's narrative, there are elements of foreshadowing that O'Nan never quite fulfills, which makes me believe that he had greater ambitions for tying this story together than he was ultimately able to live up to. And, although it is supposed to be the point of the story, Arthur never really does confront and come to terms with the life events that happen to him. Basically, his way of dealing with them is to get high and NOT deal with them, which is admirably realistic, but not the most compelling literary resolution. <br /><br />Overall, this is a quick, easy read, and enjoyable to a certain extent -- sadly just not the same extent that O'Nan's fiction usually achieves. For a better introduction to what O'Nan does so well, pick up The Night Country instead. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5096869610234610232">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_34334579">

  <a href="/user/show/83144-el" class="left imgcol" title="El"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1452263504p2/83144.jpg" alt="El" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/34334579?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 03, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/83144-el" class="user" title="El">El</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/376630.Snow_Angels" title="Paperback 9780312422769" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=20th-centurylit-late" class="actionLinkLite">20th-centurylit-late</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=yinzers-n-at" class="actionLinkLite">yinzers-n-at</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer34334579">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7655549462571259077">What <em>Staggerford</em> could not do for me, <em>Snow Angels</em> could. Instead of the make-believe Staggerford, Minnesota, O'Nan's first novel takes place north of Pittsburgh in the very real Butler, Pennsylvania, complete with Steelers fans and arguments about the correct way to pronounce "milk". Taking place in the seventies, it's another story of small-town secrets, about how lives are connected in sometimes very subtle ways, and about how everyone feels it when something happens extraordinary.<br /><br />The story is</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7655549462571259077">What <em>Staggerford</em> could not do for me, <em>Snow Angels</em> could. Instead of the make-believe Staggerford, Minnesota, O'Nan's first novel takes place north of Pittsburgh in the very real Butler, Pennsylvania, complete with Steelers fans and arguments about the correct way to pronounce "milk". Taking place in the seventies, it's another story of small-town secrets, about how lives are connected in sometimes very subtle ways, and about how everyone feels it when something happens extraordinary.<br /><br />The story is told from the perspective of one of the members of the town, Arthur Parkinson, grown up now and reflecting on what happened when he was fourteen and his life and family was falling apart. In the meantime his former babysitter's life is also falling apart, though in a strikingly different way. What happens to Annie and her family is the extraordinary that affects the entire town and makes everyone question if things will ever be normal again.<br /><br />Now becoming familiar with O'Nan's writing I recognized at least one entire chapter that was previously published as the short story <em>Finding Amy</em> in the short story collection I previously read, <em>In the Walled City</em>. It made me realize how a simple short story can be expanded upon to create a full-length novel.<br /><br />I've read some mention about <em>Snow Angels</em> coming out as a movie soon, though all I know about it is it stars Sam Rockwell, Kate Beckinsale and Drew Barrymore - while I can imagine Rockwell as a character in the book, I'm having trouble deciding how Beckinsale and Barrymore would fit in a movie version as I have yet to see either of them successfully pull off a sincerely dark character (and no, Barrymore's <em>Poison Ivy</em> does not exactly count!).<br /><br />The bottom line is for a first novel O'Nan has accomplished much and I continue to look forward to getting around to reading the rest of his books.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7655549462571259077">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span style="display:none" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer22289619">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16608312614122903256">One word that I would use to describe this book would be depressing. I should have just read the first two chapters and then the last one. The information and plot in between was really a waste of time. It seems like everyone was having relationship issues and a way to help them cope was to separate and have an affair with someone. The only two things that I could think of that were nice was the relationship between Arthur and his best friend, Warren, as well as his relationship with one of the</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16608312614122903256">One word that I would use to describe this book would be depressing. I should have just read the first two chapters and then the last one. The information and plot in between was really a waste of time. It seems like everyone was having relationship issues and a way to help them cope was to separate and have an affair with someone. The only two things that I could think of that were nice was the relationship between Arthur and his best friend, Warren, as well as his relationship with one of the twins (I have already forgot which one he went out with Lily or Lila, I think it was Lila). Both relationships were rarely highlighted when you compare it to the other topics of: Arthur's parent’s rocky relationship/divorce, Annie and Glen's relationship, Annie cheating with Brock (her best friend's husband), Annie's relationship with her daughter and then her tragic death, followed by Annie getting killed and Glen committing suicide. Again it was very depressing. <br /><br /><br /></span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <a href="/user/show/11889231-diana-lyn" class="user" title="Diana Lyn">Diana Lyn</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/1083304.Snow_Angels" title="Paperback 9780312427696" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/11889231-diana-lyn?shelf=just-okay" class="actionLinkLite">just-okay</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/11889231-diana-lyn?shelf=death" class="actionLinkLite">death</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer914303485">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3391440542563245673">I don't think I got the whole point of the book.<br /><br />As a teaser, the back of the book said that Arthur will soon find out that the death of his former babysitter links as to why his parents divorced or why their family was torn apart. I've read it and I didn't get it. I didn't get why Annie's death would've answered some of Arthur's questions.<br /><br />But if you really try and manually knot everything up, instead of just figuring out that that was how it was knotted the whole time, I'd guess that somehow, An</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3391440542563245673">I don't think I got the whole point of the book.<br /><br />As a teaser, the back of the book said that Arthur will soon find out that the death of his former babysitter links as to why his parents divorced or why their family was torn apart. I've read it and I didn't get it. I didn't get why Annie's death would've answered some of Arthur's questions.<br /><br />But if you really try and manually knot everything up, instead of just figuring out that that was how it was knotted the whole time, I'd guess that somehow, Annie's sexual trysts are the sole reason why she's dead. Still didn't give way as to why the Parkinson's life was getting miserable every turn of the page. I thought that maybe Annie being the rebel would've had an affair with Mr. Parkinson here or maybe even sexually assaulted Arthur, but there was no clear connection. Or am I just dumb?<br /><br />Also, I thought his mother was a real pain in the ass but she's just being a mother. At the beginning of the story, his mother opens up that he has to understand that it's not all his father's fault. She's fallen in love with a man that she knows will not reciprocate these feelings and somehow his father found out. In due time, I found out that they're still communicating, his father (a dick, if I may say so) still opening up to his mother. I think that O'Nan made it a little realistic by making this father contact his wife and tell her about his new girlfriend and how he's planning to marry. And then begging to be back again, *cue Taylor Swift's <strong>Should've Said No</strong>*<br /><br />Anyway, it was an okay book. I wanted to finish it up quickly so I could move on.<br /></span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1179414548">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1179414548" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/17956105-dawn-massie" class="left imgcol" title="Dawn Massie"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1362181606p2/17956105.jpg" alt="Dawn Massie" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1179414548?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 24, 2015</a>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a onclick="Effect.toggle('reviewTextContainer1179414548'); return false;" href="#">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span style="display:none" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1179414548">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer526918369558164258">Started out very promising, but ultimately disappointing. I thought this book was going to be more of a mystery, the adult Arthur coming home to learn what really lay behind the murder of his childhood babysitter. Instead we get one brief passage of the adult Arthur at the beginning of the book and then nothing. I did like the story of the childhood Arthur. I liked much less the story of Annie. I thought the death of her daughter was going to prove to be not an accident. Can a 3-year-old tie up</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText526918369558164258">Started out very promising, but ultimately disappointing. I thought this book was going to be more of a mystery, the adult Arthur coming home to learn what really lay behind the murder of his childhood babysitter. Instead we get one brief passage of the adult Arthur at the beginning of the book and then nothing. I did like the story of the childhood Arthur. I liked much less the story of Annie. I thought the death of her daughter was going to prove to be not an accident. Can a 3-year-old tie up her snowsuit hood? I don't think so. That makes it implausible that she just wandered off, but I guess that's what we're supposed to believe. And then I thought Annie's death was going to be more than what it seemed, but no. And the most disappointing thing was the ending. The story just stopped.<br /><br />The author's writing sweeps you up at points, but it's not nearly enough to overcome this bleak, unlikely, unsatisfying story.</span>

          </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/376630.Snow_Angels" title="Paperback 9780312422769" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer182896961">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7578180253879594575">This book is stunning, just as the NPR reviewer the other day suggested. It is a story of domestic life gone wrong, tangled together with the death of a little girl and the murder of two adults. The writing is so simple and beautiful, unexpectedly rendering the tragedy of the story in multiple shades of sorrow. The voice is powerful and utterly believable.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1106935189?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 17, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3592260-jill-stevenson" class="user" title="Jill Stevenson">Jill Stevenson</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/376630.Snow_Angels" title="Paperback 9780312422769" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1106935189">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6339536545011789608">I love the way Stewart O'Nan writes. These stories are bleak, for their are indeed two parallel tales being told in this novel. Set in the winter of 1974 in western Pennsylvania, Arthur Parkinson's life is upended when his parents announce they are separating. His former babysitter, Annie Marchand is separated from her husband, Glenn, who battles depression and suicidal tendencies. Their daughter, Tara, remains the thread that binds them together. When Tara drowns, Glenn's life spirals downward.</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6339536545011789608">I love the way Stewart O'Nan writes. These stories are bleak, for their are indeed two parallel tales being told in this novel. Set in the winter of 1974 in western Pennsylvania, Arthur Parkinson's life is upended when his parents announce they are separating. His former babysitter, Annie Marchand is separated from her husband, Glenn, who battles depression and suicidal tendencies. Their daughter, Tara, remains the thread that binds them together. When Tara drowns, Glenn's life spirals downward. Meanwhile, Arthur (who found Tara in the creek), tries to make sense of his life after he and his mother move from their home into an apartment. Stewart O'Nan deftly weaves the tales together, reaching a heartbreaking climax at the end of the novel. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6339536545011789608">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13051118429510363197">I don't know what to feel with this book. This is the first novel I've read from this author. I don't know if it is just the writing style of the author or is it just me not being able to grasp the story. -___- This is the story of young Arthur and his struggle to overcome issues of growing up and the divorce of his parents while at the same time on the sidelines his nanny during his younger years being having her own issues is being discussed too. I only got hook with the book on the last 50 pages of it. I nearly surrendered and stop in reading it because I really can't get on to it. I had high expectations with the book because the synopsis is good and I thought I could easily get through it because it's only like 300+ pages and having big fonts. The description of the place was hard for me to visualize. There are some point when the story seems to go uphill then it will immediately be cut and a new story line begins. I hope to find another book by this author because I would be buying it just to compare if that is how he really writes. I am not surrendering with this author. </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1345552896">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3358349175326430599">It's 'Affliction' pared-down; less philosophical; less interested in making sense of the 'entire human condition' and, instead, focused on making sense of these particular characters' lives. The highest compliment I can pay the book is that it made me want to seek-out O'Nan's short story collection. He reminds me a lot of early Ethan Canin and there are some similarities, I think, between 'Snow Angels' and Jim Shepard's first novel, 'Flights.' My only complaint is how bleak everything is--which,</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3358349175326430599">It's 'Affliction' pared-down; less philosophical; less interested in making sense of the 'entire human condition' and, instead, focused on making sense of these particular characters' lives. The highest compliment I can pay the book is that it made me want to seek-out O'Nan's short story collection. He reminds me a lot of early Ethan Canin and there are some similarities, I think, between 'Snow Angels' and Jim Shepard's first novel, 'Flights.' My only complaint is how bleak everything is--which, normally, I love. But there's something here approaching 'one-note-ness' that rings just a tad too much for me. But, very very haunting. O'Nan is the Man.</span>

      <a href="/user/show/7685737-angelique" class="user" title="Angelique">Angelique</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer972321224">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14924234069474568045">This book left me unsatisfied. I listened to it on audiobook and enjoyed the narrator. The writing is straightforward and the characters and situations were compelling enough but it didn't really go anywhere. Two big tragedies occur but there's very little commentary on either. There's no chance for redemption for any of the characters and no message imparted. I felt a little ripped off by that and the lack of ending. The reader is left holding the bag with all these yucky feelings and nothing m</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14924234069474568045">This book left me unsatisfied. I listened to it on audiobook and enjoyed the narrator. The writing is straightforward and the characters and situations were compelling enough but it didn't really go anywhere. Two big tragedies occur but there's very little commentary on either. There's no chance for redemption for any of the characters and no message imparted. I felt a little ripped off by that and the lack of ending. The reader is left holding the bag with all these yucky feelings and nothing meaningful to do with them. It's narrated by an adult version of a child involved in the story and yet the adult doesn't really do much more than tell the story. He doesn't synthesize the different plots together to help us figure out what to make of it all. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14924234069474568045">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13487034748524137443">Stewart O'Nan has become one of my favorite authors. This story and "Last Night at the Lobster" are very different but have similar themes throughout. Both take place in depressed areas in the Northeast during winter when it's snowy, cold and bleak. Nothing earthshattering happens in "The Lobster" but it's remarkable how he brings mundane characters with mundane existences to life. More happens in "Snow Angels" but even without the suspense that builds throughout the book, it would have been a f</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13487034748524137443">Stewart O'Nan has become one of my favorite authors. This story and "Last Night at the Lobster" are very different but have similar themes throughout. Both take place in depressed areas in the Northeast during winter when it's snowy, cold and bleak. Nothing earthshattering happens in "The Lobster" but it's remarkable how he brings mundane characters with mundane existences to life. More happens in "Snow Angels" but even without the suspense that builds throughout the book, it would have been a fine read. I highly recommend both books.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer540181444018504759">MY TAKE:<br />“An intimate tale of the price of love and belonging, told in a spare, translucent, and unexpectedly tender voice.” It was a touching novel – reminiscent in tonality of “All About Lulu,” it is an insidious telling that sneaks its way into your heart.   <span style="display:none" id="freeText540181444018504759">MY TAKE:<br />“An intimate tale of the price of love and belonging, told in a spare, translucent, and unexpectedly tender voice.” It was a touching novel – reminiscent in tonality of “All About Lulu,” it is an insidious telling that sneaks its way into your heart. The stories and the paths of these people’s lives are … dark, depressing, unending, honest and real. How their lives interconnect, weaving through each other, all affected in some way by the death of Tara and Annie. I see pieces of myself and Rachel in Annie, bits of Paul in Glenn, my own father in Henry. It made me think of U, and the choices that we make in our lives and how they can tear us apart. It reminded me a lot of Cashmere, of grasping at straws trying to make sense of things. The moments that struck me: Arthur and Lila, in his quest for something good and bright in a life fallen apart; Glenn’s descent into fanatical religion and grief, causing him to take such drastic measures to end his pain; Mr. C’s quest for the perfect tornado, his own personal vision of perfection; Annie’s temper, built off her frustration and dissatisfaction with her life; the motel for a few stolen moments of bliss, knowing it was wrong; and the snow angels, and trying to leave your mark in the world, no matter how fleeting.<br /><br />“Because though it was already happening to me, I could not see how I would ever come to hate the people I loved. Yet at the same time I could do nothing to stop it, and that would not change for a very long time.”<br /><br />nothing is deader than this small town main street,<br />where the venerable elm sickens, and hardens<br />with tarred cement, where no leaf<br />is born, or falls, or resists till winter.<br />	but I remember its former fertility,<br />	how everything came out clearly<br />	in the hour of credulity<br />	and young summer, when this street<br />	was already somewhat overshaded,<br />	and here at the altar of surrender,<br />	I met you,<br />	the death of thirst in my brief flesh. – Robert Lowell<br /></span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_31657860">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/31657860" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1480127-karen" class="left imgcol" title="Karen"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1220275718p2/1480127.jpg" alt="Karen" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/31657860?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 31, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1480127-karen" class="user" title="Karen">Karen</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/376630.Snow_Angels" title="Paperback 9780312422769" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer31657860">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10421122446396099461">My Review of Snow Angels by Stewart O’Nan<br /><br />This is a story that links two families, almost indirectly, by a tragedy that affects them in enormously painful ways. Set in a rural community in Pennsylvania in mid-1970, the story is built around the lives of the two main characters, Arthur Parkinson and Annie Marchand. Arthur, who is the narrator of the chapters about his part in this heartbreaking story, is a 14-year-old high school student who is dealing with his family’s slowly decaying break up. A</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10421122446396099461">My Review of Snow Angels by Stewart O’Nan<br /><br />This is a story that links two families, almost indirectly, by a tragedy that affects them in enormously painful ways. Set in a rural community in Pennsylvania in mid-1970, the story is built around the lives of the two main characters, Arthur Parkinson and Annie Marchand. Arthur, who is the narrator of the chapters about his part in this heartbreaking story, is a 14-year-old high school student who is dealing with his family’s slowly decaying break up. At the same time, a narrator who gives us the picture of her dismal, failing marriage and careless lifestyle, tells Annie’s chaotic story. <br /><br />Arthur and his older sister Astrid are the children of parents who are selfish and immature, putting their needs ahead of their children. As Arthur’s mother decides to divorce his father, the life Arthur knows begins to change. Moving and their resulting socio-economic situation only add to Arthur’s problems as he tries to confront his involvement in Annie’s story. Adolescence, confusion, fear, and torment all play into Arthur’s mental state during this time. The events in his life during these years are only overshadowed by the awful part that involves Arthur in Annie’s heartbreaking calamity. Whereas Annie Marchand was once the delightful babysitter to Arthur and Astrid, she soon inadvertently becomes the center of many of the problems for this family and especially Arthur.<br /><br />Annie, meanwhile, grows up to marry Glenn Marchand and her imprudent and neglectful acts soon result in her leaving Glenn, despite how it may effect her daughter, Tara. Although Glenn tries in his own somewhat feeble way to reconcile with Annie, who he loves, she rejects his efforts. In fact, she goes as far as to having an affair with Brock who is one of her own friend’s boyfriends. Annie, proves to be even more selfish than one can imagine to the point that she neglects even Tara. This results in tragic consequences that lead to the beginning, and the end, of this tormented tale. Annie’s future is one that she herself brings about through her actions. And yet, O’Nan’s treatment of Annie’s character can still leave one with sympathy for her. <br /><br />In the end, we find Arthur questioning still what really has gone on and how things happened. Arthur feels that perhaps if he concentrates on the details of the past few years that are described in the book, that perhaps he “will finally understand everything that happened back then” and yet he goes on to say that he knows he can’t. This leaves us with great sympathy for Arthur who turns out to be somewhat of an innocent bystander to all that goes on around him due in fact to all the other characters’ actions.<br /><br />Snow Angels by Stewart O’Nan is one of his earliest works. Recently having previewed his soon to be released Songs for the Missing and going back to read Last Night at the Lobster, I wasn’t sure what to expect with Snow Angels. I was pleasantly relieved to find that Snow Angels fell in line with my opinion of O’Nan as based on Last Night at the Lobster, instead of the extremely disappointing Songs for the Missing. With Snow Angels Stewart O’Nan gives us the same working class characterizations that made me love his “Lobster” book and allows the reader to relate to the story and want to finish reading it without stopping. This is a story that will stay with the reader for a long time, as it will with me.<br /><br />© Karen D. Haney, 2008 for Curled Up With A Good Book at <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.curledup.com" target="_blank">www.curledup.com</a><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10421122446396099461">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
